   <h1 class="firstHeading" id="firstHeading" lang="en">
    God
   </h1>
   <div class="mw-body-content" id="bodyContent">
    <div id="siteSub">
     From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia
    </div>
    <div id="contentSub">
     <span class="mw-redirectedfrom">
      (Redirected from
      <a href="/w/index.php?title=Attributes_of_God&amp;redirect=no" title="Attributes of God">
       Attributes of God
      </a>
      )
     </span>
    </div>
    <div class="mw-jump" id="jump-to-nav">

      navigation

    <div class="mw-content-ltr" dir="ltr" id="mw-content-text" lang="en">
     <div class="hatnote">
      This article is about the concept "God" in the context of
      <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">
       monotheism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Henotheism" title="Henotheism">
       henotheism
      </a>
      .  For the general concept of "a god", see
      <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">
       Deity
      </a>
      .  For God in specific religions, see
      <a href="#In_specific_religions">
       #In specific religions
      </a>
      .  For discussion of the existence of God, see
      <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">
       Existence of God
      </a>
      .
     </div>

            </a>
           </li>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/God_complex" title="God complex">
             God complex
            </a>
           </li>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/God_gene" title="God gene">
             God gene
            </a>
           </li>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">
             Theology
            </a>
           </li>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/Ontology" title="Ontology">
             Ontology
            </a>
           </li>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_evil" title="Problem of evil">
             Problem of evil
            </a>
            (
            <a href="/wiki/Theodicy" title="Theodicy">
             theodicy
            </a>
            )
           </li>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">
             Religion
            </a>
            <ul>
             <li>
              <a href="/wiki/Philosophy_of_religion" title="Philosophy of religion">
               philosophy
              </a>
             </li>
             <li>
              <a href="/wiki/Religious_text" title="Religious text">
               texts
              </a>
             </li>
            </ul>
           </li>
           <li>
            <a href="/wiki/Portrayals_of_God_in_popular_media" title="Portrayals of God in popular media">
             Portrayals of God in popular media
            </a>
           </li>
          </ul>
         </div>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>
      <tr>
       <td style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;">
        <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">
         <ul>
          <li class="nv-view">
           <a href="/wiki/Template:God" title="Template:God">
            <span title="View this template">
             v
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-talk">
           <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:God" title="Template talk:God">
            <span title="Discuss this template">
             t
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
          <li class="nv-edit">
           <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:God&amp;action=edit">
            <span title="Edit this template">
             e
            </span>
           </a>
          </li>
         </ul>
        </div>
       </td>
      </tr>
     </table>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">
       monotheism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Henotheism" title="Henotheism">
       henotheism
      </a>
      ,
      <b>
       God
      </b>
      is conceived as the
      <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Being" title="Supreme Being">
       Supreme Being
      </a>
      and principal object of
      <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">
       faith
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinburne_1-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Swinburne-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a href="/wiki/Conceptions_of_God" title="Conceptions of God">
       concept of God
      </a>
      as described by
      <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">
       theologians
      </a>
      commonly includes the attributes of
      <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">
       omniscience
      </a>
      (infinite knowledge),
      <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">
       omnipotence
      </a>
      (unlimited power),
      <a href="/wiki/Omnipresence" title="Omnipresence">
       omnipresence
      </a>
      (present everywhere),
      <a href="/wiki/Omnibenevolence" title="Omnibenevolence">
       omnibenevolence
      </a>
      (perfect goodness),
      <a href="/wiki/Divine_simplicity" title="Divine simplicity">
       divine simplicity
      </a>
      , and eternal and necessary existence. In
      <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">
       theism
      </a>
      , God is the
      <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">
       creator
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/God_the_Sustainer" title="God the Sustainer">
       sustainer
      </a>
      of the universe, while in
      <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">
       deism
      </a>
      , God is the creator, but not the sustainer, of the universe.
      <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">
       Monotheism
      </a>
      is the belief in the existence of one God or in the oneness of God. In
      <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">
       pantheism
      </a>
      , God is the universe itself. In
      <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">
       atheism
      </a>
      , God does not exist, while God is deemed unknown or unknowable within the context of
      <a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">
       agnosticism
      </a>
      . God has also been conceived as being
      <a href="/wiki/Incorporeality" title="Incorporeality">
       incorporeal
      </a>
      (immaterial), a
      <a href="/wiki/Personal_god" title="Personal god">
       personal
      </a>
      being, the source of all
      <a href="/wiki/Moral_obligation" title="Moral obligation">
       moral obligation
      </a>
      , and the "greatest conceivable existent".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinburne_1-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Swinburne-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Many notable
      <a href="/wiki/Medieval_philosophy" title="Medieval philosophy">
       medieval philosophers
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Modern_philosophy" title="Modern philosophy">
       modern philosophers
      </a>
      have developed arguments for and against the
      <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">
       existence of God
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Platinga_2-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Platinga-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There are many
      <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God" title="Names of God">
       names for God
      </a>
      , and different names are attached to different cultural ideas about God's identity and attributes. In the ancient Egyptian era of
      <a href="/wiki/Atenism" title="Atenism">
       Atenism
      </a>
      , possibly the earliest recorded monotheistic religion, this deity was called
      <a href="/wiki/Aten" title="Aten">
       Aten
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-3">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        3
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      premised on being the one "true" Supreme Being and Creator of the Universe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">
       <a href="#cite_note-4">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        4
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In the
      <a href="/wiki/Hebrew_Bible" title="Hebrew Bible">
       Hebrew Bible
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">
       Judaism
      </a>
      , "He Who Is," "
      <a href="/wiki/I_Am_that_I_Am" title="I Am that I Am">
       I Am that I Am
      </a>
      ", and the
      <a href="/wiki/Tetragrammaton" title="Tetragrammaton">
       tetragrammaton
      </a>
      YHWH are used as names of God, while
      <a href="/wiki/Yahweh" title="Yahweh">
       Yahweh
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Jehovah" title="Jehovah">
       Jehovah
      </a>
      are sometimes used in Christianity as vocalizations of YHWH. In Judaism, it is common to refer to God by the titular
      <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Judaism" title="Names of God in Judaism">
       names
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Elohim" title="Elohim">
       Elohim
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adonai" title="Adonai">
       Adonai
      </a>
      , the latter of which is believed by some scholars to descend from the Egyptian Aten.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-freud_5-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-freud-5">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        5
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">
       <a href="#cite_note-6">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        6
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7">
       <a href="#cite_note-7">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        7
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">
       <a href="#cite_note-8">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        8
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">
       <a href="#cite_note-9">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        9
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">
       Islam
      </a>
      , the name
      <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">
       Allah
      </a>
      , "Al-El," or "Al-Elah" ("the God") is used, while Muslims also have a
      <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Islam" title="Names of God in Islam">
       multitude of titular names
      </a>
      for God. In
      <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">
       Hinduism
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Brahman" title="Brahman">
       Brahman
      </a>
      is often considered a
      <a href="/wiki/Monism" title="Monism">
       monistic
      </a>
      deity.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">
       <a href="#cite_note-10">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        10
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Other religions have names for God, for instance,
      <a href="/wiki/God_in_the_Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="God in the Bah' Faith">
       Baha
      </a>
      in the
      <a href="/wiki/Bah%C3%A1%27%C3%AD_Faith" title="Bah' Faith">
       Bah' Faith
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11">
       <a href="#cite_note-11">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        11
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Waheguru" title="Waheguru">
       Waheguru
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">
       Sikhism
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">
       <a href="#cite_note-12">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        12
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Ahura_Mazda" title="Ahura Mazda">
       Ahura Mazda
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Zoroastrianism" title="Zoroastrianism">
       Zoroastrianism
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">
       <a href="#cite_note-13">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        13
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      The many different conceptions of God, and competing claims as to God's characteristics, aims, and actions, have led to the development of ideas of
      <a href="/wiki/Omnitheism" title="Omnitheism">
       omnitheism
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Pandeism" title="Pandeism">
       pandeism
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lataster_14-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Lataster-14">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        14
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawe_15-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dawe-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      or a
      <a href="/wiki/Perennial_philosophy" title="Perennial philosophy">
       perennial philosophy
      </a>
      , which postulates that there is one underlying theological truth, of which all religions express a partial understanding, and as to which "the devout in the various great world religions are in fact worshipping that one God, but through different, overlapping concepts or mental images of him."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">
       <a href="#cite_note-16">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        16
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
     </p>
