     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Etymology_and_usage">
       Etymology and usage
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Creation_of_the_Sun_and_Moon_face_detail.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3064" data-file-width="2184" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Creation_of_the_Sun_and_Moon_face_detail.jpg/170px-Creation_of_the_Sun_and_Moon_face_detail.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Creation_of_the_Sun_and_Moon_face_detail.jpg/255px-Creation_of_the_Sun_and_Moon_face_detail.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d0/Creation_of_the_Sun_and_Moon_face_detail.jpg/340px-Creation_of_the_Sun_and_Moon_face_detail.jpg 2x" width="170"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Creation_of_the_Sun_and_Moon_face_detail.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Detail of
        <a href="/wiki/Sistine_Chapel" title="Sistine Chapel">
         Sistine Chapel
        </a>
        fresco
        <i>
         Creation of the Sun and Moon
        </i>
        by
        <a href="/wiki/Michelangelo" title="Michelangelo">
         Michelangelo
        </a>
        (c. 1512), a well-known example of the depiction of
        <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father_in_Western_art" title="God the Father in Western art">
         God the Father in Western art
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/God_(word)" title="God (word)">
       God (word)
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The earliest written form of the Germanic word
      <i>
       God
      </i>
      (always, in this usage,
      <a href="/wiki/Capitalization_in_English" title="Capitalization in English">
       capitalized
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">
       <a href="#cite_note-17">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        17
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ) comes from the 6th-century
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
       Christian
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Codex_Argenteus" title="Codex Argenteus">
       Codex Argenteus
      </a>
      . The English word itself is derived from the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Germanic" title="Proto-Germanic">
       Proto-Germanic
      </a>
      * uan. The reconstructed
      <a href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_language" title="Proto-Indo-European language">
       Proto-Indo-European
      </a>
      form
      <span class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space: nowrap" title="Proto-Indo-European language">
       * hu-t-m
      </span>
      was likely based on the root
      <span class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space: nowrap" title="Proto-Indo-European language">
       * hau()-
      </span>
      , which meant either "to call" or "to invoke".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">
       <a href="#cite_note-18">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        18
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The Germanic words for
      <i>
       God
      </i>
      were originally
      <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">
       neuter
      </a>
      applying to both gendersbut during the process of the
      <a href="/wiki/Christianization" title="Christianization">
       Christianization
      </a>
      of the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanic_people" title="Germanic people">
       Germanic peoples
      </a>
      from their indigenous
      <a href="/wiki/Germanic_paganism" title="Germanic paganism">
       Germanic paganism
      </a>
      , the words became a
      <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_gender" title="Grammatical gender">
       masculine syntactic form
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BARNHART323_19-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-BARNHART323-19">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        19
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In the
      <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">
       English language
      </a>
      , the capitalized form of
      <i>
       God
      </i>
      continues to represent a distinction between monotheistic "God" and "gods" in
      <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">
       polytheism
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">
       <a href="#cite_note-20">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        20
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">
       <a href="#cite_note-21">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        21
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The English word
      <i>
       God
      </i>
      and its counterparts in other languages are normally used for any and all conceptions and, in spite of significant differences between religions, the term remains an English translation common to all. The same holds for Hebrew
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/El_(god)" title="El (god)">
        El
       </a>
      </i>
      , but
      <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Judaism" title="Names of God in Judaism">
       in Judaism
      </a>
      , God is also given a proper name, the
      <a href="/wiki/Tetragrammaton" title="Tetragrammaton">
       tetragrammaton
      </a>
      YHWH, in origin the name of an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Edomite" title="Edomite">
       Edomite
      </a>
      or
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Midianite" title="Midianite">
       Midianite
      </a>
      deity,
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Yahweh_(Canaanite_deity)" title="Yahweh (Canaanite deity)">
       Yahweh
      </a>
      . In many translations of the
      <a href="/wiki/Bible" title="Bible">
       Bible
      </a>
      , when the word
      <i>
       LORD
      </i>
      is in all capitals, it signifies that the word represents the tetragrammaton.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Barton2006_22-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Barton2006-22">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        22
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <i>
       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/All%C4%81h" title="Allh">
        Allh
       </a>
      </i>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
       Arabic
      </a>
      :
      <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">
       
      </span>
      ) is the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arabic" title="Arabic">
       Arabic
      </a>
      term with no
      <a href="/wiki/Plural" title="Plural">
       plural
      </a>
      used by Muslims and Arabic speaking Christians and Jews meaning "The God" (with a capital G), while "
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/%CA%BEil%C4%81h" title="ilh">
       ilh
      </a>
      " (
      <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language">
       Arabic
      </a>
      :
      <span dir="rtl" lang="ar">
       <b>
        
       </b>
      </span>
      ) is the term used for a deity or a god in general.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">
       <a href="#cite_note-23">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        23
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">
       <a href="#cite_note-24">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        24
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">
       <a href="#cite_note-25">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        25
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      God may also be given a proper name in monotheistic currents of Hinduism which emphasize the
      <a href="/wiki/Personal_god" title="Personal god">
       personal nature of God
      </a>
      , with early references to his name as
      <a href="/wiki/Krishna" title="Krishna">
       Krishna
      </a>
      -
      <a href="/wiki/Vasudeva" title="Vasudeva">
       Vasudeva
      </a>
      in
      <a href="/wiki/Bhagavata" title="Bhagavata">
       Bhagavata
      </a>
      or later
      <a href="/wiki/Vishnu" title="Vishnu">
       Vishnu
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hari" title="Hari">
       Hari
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hastings541_26-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Hastings541-26">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        26
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="General_conceptions">
       General conceptions
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Conceptions_of_God" title="Conceptions of God">
       Conceptions of God
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      There is no clear consensus on the nature of God.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">
       <a href="#cite_note-27">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        27
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Abrahamic_conceptions_of_God" title="Abrahamic conceptions of God">
       Abrahamic conceptions of God
      </a>
      include the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotheistic" title="Monotheistic">
       monotheistic
      </a>
      definition of God in
      <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">
       Judaism
      </a>
      , the
      <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">
       trinitarian
      </a>
      view of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Christians" title="Christians">
       Christians
      </a>
      , and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_concept_of_God" title="Islamic concept of God">
       Islamic concept of God
      </a>
      . The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dharmic_religions" title="Dharmic religions">
       dharmic religions
      </a>
      differ in their view of the divine: views of
      <a href="/wiki/God_in_Hinduism" title="God in Hinduism">
       God in Hinduism
      </a>
      vary by region, sect, and caste, ranging from monotheistic to polytheistic to atheistic. Divinity was
      <a href="/wiki/God_in_Buddhism" title="God in Buddhism">
       recognized
      </a>
      by the historical Buddha, particularly
      <a href="/wiki/%C5%9Aakra_(Buddhism)" title="akra (Buddhism)">
       akra
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Brahma_(Buddhism)" title="Brahma (Buddhism)">
       Brahma
      </a>
      . However, other sentient beings, including gods, can at best only play a supportive role in one's personal path to salvation. Conceptions of God in the latter developments of the
      <a href="/wiki/Mahayana" title="Mahayana">
       Mahayana
      </a>
      tradition give a more prominent place to notions of the divine.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Oneness">
       Oneness
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">
       Monotheism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Henotheism" title="Henotheism">
       Henotheism
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="468" data-file-width="520" height="198" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/220px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/330px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b3/Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg/440px-Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Shield-Trinity-Scutum-Fidei-English.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The
        <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">
         Trinity
        </a>
        is the belief in Christianity that God is one God in three persons:
        <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">
         God the Father
        </a>
        ,
        <a href="/wiki/God_the_Son" title="God the Son">
         God the Son
        </a>
        (
        <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">
         Jesus
        </a>
        ), and
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/God_the_Holy_Spirit" title="God the Holy Spirit">
         God the Holy Spirit
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Monotheism" title="Monotheism">
       Monotheists
      </a>
      hold that there is only one god, and may claim that the one true god is worshiped in different religions under different names. The view that all theists actually worship the same god, whether they know it or not, is especially emphasized in
      <a href="/wiki/Hinduism" title="Hinduism">
       Hinduism
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">
       <a href="#cite_note-28">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        28
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Sikhism" title="Sikhism">
       Sikhism
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">
       <a href="#cite_note-29">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        29
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
       Christianity
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Doctrine_of_the_Trinity" title="Doctrine of the Trinity">
       doctrine of the Trinity
      </a>
      describes God as one God in three persons. The Trinity is composed of
      <a href="/wiki/God_the_Father" title="God the Father">
       God the Father
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/God_the_Son" title="God the Son">
       God the Son
      </a>
      (
      <a href="/wiki/Jesus" title="Jesus">
       Jesus
      </a>
      ), and
      <a href="/wiki/Holy_Spirit_(Christianity)" title="Holy Spirit (Christianity)">
       God the Holy Spirit
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">
       <a href="#cite_note-30">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        30
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">
       Islam
      </a>
      's most fundamental concept is
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/Tawhid" title="Tawhid">
        tawhid
       </a>
      </i>
      (meaning "oneness" or "uniqueness"). God is described in the
      <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">
       Quran
      </a>
      as: "Say: He is Allah, the One and Only; Allah, the Eternal, Absolute; He begetteth not, nor is He begotten; And there is none like unto Him."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">
       <a href="#cite_note-31">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        31
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">
       <a href="#cite_note-32">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        32
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Muslims repudiate the Christian doctrine of the
      <a href="/wiki/Trinity" title="Trinity">
       Trinity
      </a>
      and divinity of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Islamic_view_of_Jesus" title="Islamic view of Jesus">
       Jesus
      </a>
      , comparing it to
      <a href="/wiki/Polytheism" title="Polytheism">
       polytheism
      </a>
      . In Islam, God is beyond all comprehension or equal and does not resemble any of his creations in any way. Thus,
      <a href="/wiki/Muslim" title="Muslim">
       Muslims
      </a>
      are not
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Iconodules" title="Iconodules">
       iconodules
      </a>
      , and are not expected to visualize God.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">
       <a href="#cite_note-33">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        33
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Henotheism" title="Henotheism">
       Henotheism
      </a>
      is the belief and worship of a single god while accepting the existence or possible existence of other
      <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">
       deities
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34">
       <a href="#cite_note-34">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        34
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Theism.2C_deism_and_pantheism">
       Theism, deism and pantheism
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">
       Theism
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">
       Deism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">
       Pantheism
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">
       Theism
      </a>
      generally holds that God exists realistically, objectively, and independently of human thought; that God created and sustains everything; that God is omnipotent and eternal; and that God is personal and interacting with the universe through, for example,
      <a href="/wiki/Religious_experience" title="Religious experience">
       religious experience
      </a>
      and the prayers of humans.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smart_35-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-smart-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Theism holds that God is both transcendent and immanent; thus, God is simultaneously infinite and in some way present in the affairs of the world.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lemos_36-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-lemos-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Not all theists subscribe to all the above propositions, but they usually subscribe to one or more of them (see, by way of comparison,
      <a href="/wiki/Family_resemblance" title="Family resemblance">
       family resemblance
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-smart_35-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-smart-35">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        35
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Catholic theology holds that God is
      <a href="/wiki/Divine_simplicity" title="Divine simplicity">
       infinitely simple
      </a>
      and is not involuntarily subject to time. Most theists hold that God is omnipotent, omniscient, and benevolent, although this belief raises questions about God's responsibility for evil and suffering in the world. Some theists ascribe to God a self-conscious or purposeful limiting of omnipotence, omniscience, or benevolence.
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Open_Theism" title="Open Theism">
       Open Theism
      </a>
      , by contrast, asserts that, due to the nature of time, God's omniscience does not mean the deity can predict the future.
      <i>
       Theism
      </i>
      is sometimes used to refer in general to any belief in a god or gods, i.e., monotheism or polytheism.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-philosofrelGlossthe_37-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-philosofrelGlossthe-37">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        37
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TFDtheism_38-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-TFDtheism-38">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        38
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Deism" title="Deism">
       Deism
      </a>
      holds that God is wholly
      <a href="/wiki/Transcendence_(religion)" title="Transcendence (religion)">
       transcendent
      </a>
      : God exists, but does not intervene in the world beyond what was necessary to create it.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lemos_36-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-lemos-36">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        36
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In this view, God is not
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anthropomorphic" title="Anthropomorphic">
       anthropomorphic
      </a>
      and does not literally answer prayers or cause miracles to occur. Common in Deism is a belief that God has no interest in humanity and may not even be aware of humanity.
      <a href="/wiki/Pandeism" title="Pandeism">
       Pandeism
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Panendeism" title="Panendeism">
       Panendeism
      </a>
      , respectively, combine Deism with the Pantheistic or Panentheistic beliefs discussed below.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawe_15-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dawe-15">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        15
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-39">
       <a href="#cite_note-39">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        39
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40">
       <a href="#cite_note-40">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        40
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Pandeism is proposed to explain as to Deism why God would create a universe and then abandon it,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller_41-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fuller-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and as to Pantheism, the origin and purpose of the universe.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fuller_41-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Fuller-41">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        41
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42">
       <a href="#cite_note-42">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        42
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Pantheism" title="Pantheism">
       Pantheism
      </a>
      holds that God is the universe and the universe is God, whereas
      <a href="/wiki/Panentheism" title="Panentheism">
       Panentheism
      </a>
      holds that God contains, but is not identical to, the Universe.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (July 2013)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
      It is also the view of the
      <a href="/wiki/Liberal_Catholic_Church" title="Liberal Catholic Church">
       Liberal Catholic Church
      </a>
      ;
      <a href="/wiki/Theosophy" title="Theosophy">
       Theosophy
      </a>
      ; some views of Hinduism except
      <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">
       Vaishnavism
      </a>
      , which believes in
      <a href="/wiki/Panentheism" title="Panentheism">
       panentheism
      </a>
      ; Sikhism; some divisions of
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Neopaganism" title="Neopaganism">
       Neopaganism
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Taoism" title="Taoism">
       Taoism
      </a>
      , along with many varying denominations and individuals within denominations.
      <a href="/wiki/Kabbalah" title="Kabbalah">
       Kabbalah
      </a>
      , Jewish mysticism, paints a pantheistic/panentheistic view of Godwhich has wide acceptance in
      <a href="/wiki/Hasidic_Judaism" title="Hasidic Judaism">
       Hasidic Judaism
      </a>
      , particularly from their founder
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Israel_ben_Eliezer" title="Israel ben Eliezer">
       The Baal Shem Tov
      </a>
      but only as an addition to the Jewish view of a personal god, not in the original pantheistic sense that denies or limits persona to God.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Other_concepts">
       Other concepts
      </span>
     </h3>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Dystheism" title="Dystheism">
       Dystheism
      </a>
      , which is related to
      <a href="/wiki/Theodicy" title="Theodicy">
       theodicy
      </a>
      , is a form of theism which holds that God is either not wholly good or is fully malevolent as a consequence of the
      <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_evil" title="Problem of evil">
       problem of evil
      </a>
      . One such example comes from
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dostoevsky" title="Dostoevsky">
       Dostoevsky
      </a>
      's
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Brothers_Karamazov" title="The Brothers Karamazov">
        The Brothers Karamazov
       </a>
      </i>
      , in which Ivan Karamazov rejects God on the grounds that he allows children to suffer.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43">
       <a href="#cite_note-43">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        43
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Another example would be
      <a href="/wiki/Theistic_Satanism" title="Theistic Satanism">
       Theistic Satanism
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      In modern times, some more abstract concepts have been developed, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Process_theology" title="Process theology">
       process theology
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Open_theism" title="Open theism">
       open theism
      </a>
      . The contemporaneous French philosopher
      <a href="/wiki/Michel_Henry" title="Michel Henry">
       Michel Henry
      </a>
      has however proposed a
      <a href="/wiki/Phenomenological_definition_of_God" title="Phenomenological definition of God">
       phenomenological approach and definition of God
      </a>
      as
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phenomenology_(religion)" title="Phenomenology (religion)">
       phenomenological
      </a>
      essence of
      <a href="/wiki/Phenomenological_life" title="Phenomenological life">
       Life
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44">
       <a href="#cite_note-44">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        44
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      God has also been conceived as being
      <a href="/wiki/Incorporeality" title="Incorporeality">
       incorporeal
      </a>
      (immaterial), a
      <a href="/wiki/Personal_god" title="Personal god">
       personal
      </a>
      being, the source of all
      <a href="/wiki/Moral_obligation" title="Moral obligation">
       moral obligation
      </a>
      , and the "greatest conceivable existent".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinburne_1-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Swinburne-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These attributes were all supported to varying degrees by the early
      <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">
       Jewish
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
       Christian
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">
       Muslim
      </a>
      theologian philosophers, including
      <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">
       Maimonides
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_45-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Edwards-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">
       Augustine of Hippo
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_45-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Edwards-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">
       Al-Ghazali
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Platinga_2-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Platinga-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      respectively.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Non-theistic_views_of_God">
       Non-theistic views of God
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_origin_of_religions" title="Evolutionary origin of religions">
       Evolutionary origin of religions
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Evolutionary_psychology_of_religion" title="Evolutionary psychology of religion">
       Evolutionary psychology of religion
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Nontheism" title="Nontheism">
       Nontheism
      </a>
      holds that the universe can be explained without any reference to the supernatural, or to a supernatural being. Some non-theists avoid the concept of God, whilst accepting that it is significant to many; other non-theists understand God as a symbol of human values and aspirations. The nineteenth-century English
      <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">
       atheist
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Charles_Bradlaugh" title="Charles Bradlaugh">
       Charles Bradlaugh
      </a>
      declared that he refused to say "There is no God", because "the word 'God' is to me a sound conveying no clear or distinct affirmation";
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46">
       <a href="#cite_note-46">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        46
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      he said more specifically that he disbelieved in the Christian god.
      <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Jay_Gould" title="Stephen Jay Gould">
       Stephen Jay Gould
      </a>
      proposed an approach dividing the world of philosophy into what he called "
      <a href="/wiki/Non-overlapping_magisteria" title="Non-overlapping magisteria">
       non-overlapping magisteria
      </a>
      " (NOMA). In this view, questions of the
      <a href="/wiki/Supernatural" title="Supernatural">
       supernatural
      </a>
      , such as those relating to the
      <a href="/wiki/Existence" title="Existence">
       existence
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Nature" title="Nature">
       nature
      </a>
      of God, are
      <a href="/wiki/Metaphysics" title="Metaphysics">
       non
      </a>
      -
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Empirical" title="Empirical">
       empirical
      </a>
      and are the proper domain of
      <a href="/wiki/Theology" title="Theology">
       theology
      </a>
      . The methods of science should then be used to answer any empirical question about the natural world, and theology should be used to answer questions about ultimate meaning and moral value. In this view, the perceived lack of any empirical footprint from the magisterium of the supernatural onto natural events makes science the sole player in the natural world.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47">
       <a href="#cite_note-47">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        47
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Another view, advanced by
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Dawkins" title="Richard Dawkins">
       Richard Dawkins
      </a>
      , is that the existence of God is an empirical question, on the grounds that "a universe with a god would be a completely different kind of universe from one without, and it would be a scientific difference."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawkins_48-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dawkins-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Carl_Sagan" title="Carl Sagan">
       Carl Sagan
      </a>
      argued that the doctrine of a Creator of the Universe was difficult to prove or disprove and that the only conceivable scientific discovery that could disprove the existence of a Creator would be the discovery that the universe is infinitely old.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49">
       <a href="#cite_note-49">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        49
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Hawking" title="Stephen Hawking">
       Stephen Hawking
      </a>
      and co-author
      <a href="/wiki/Leonard_Mlodinow" title="Leonard Mlodinow">
       Leonard Mlodinow
      </a>
      state in their book,
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/The_Grand_Design_(book)" title="The Grand Design (book)">
        The Grand Design
       </a>
      </i>
      , that it is reasonable to ask who or what created the universe, but if the answer is God, then the question has merely been deflected to that of who created God. Both authors claim however, that it is possible to answer these questions purely within the realm of science, and without invoking any divine beings.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50">
       <a href="#cite_note-50">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        50
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Neuroscientist Michael Nikoletseas has proposed that questions of the existence of God are no different from questions of natural sciences. Following a biological comparative approach, he concludes that it is highly probable that God exists, and, although not visible, it is possible that we know some of his attributes.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51">
       <a href="#cite_note-51">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        51
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Anthropomorphism">
       Anthropomorphism
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MarianartintheCatholicChurchjf0254_02.JPG">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4608" data-file-width="3456" height="200" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/MarianartintheCatholicChurchjf0254_02.JPG/150px-MarianartintheCatholicChurchjf0254_02.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/MarianartintheCatholicChurchjf0254_02.JPG/225px-MarianartintheCatholicChurchjf0254_02.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/MarianartintheCatholicChurchjf0254_02.JPG/300px-MarianartintheCatholicChurchjf0254_02.JPG 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MarianartintheCatholicChurchjf0254_02.JPG" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        Depiction of God in the Catholic Church, Latin America
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Anthropomorphism" title="Anthropomorphism">
       Anthropomorphism
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Pascal_Boyer" title="Pascal Boyer">
       Pascal Boyer
      </a>
      argues that while there is a wide array of supernatural concepts found around the world, in general, supernatural beings tend to behave much like people. The construction of gods and spirits like persons is one of the best known traits of religion. He cites examples from
      <a href="/wiki/Greek_mythology" title="Greek mythology">
       Greek mythology
      </a>
      , which is, in his opinion, more like a modern
      <a href="/wiki/Soap_opera" title="Soap opera">
       soap opera
      </a>
      than other religious systems.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-boyer_52-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-boyer-52">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        52
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Bertrand_du_Castel" title="Bertrand du Castel">
       Bertrand du Castel
      </a>
      and Timothy Jurgensen demonstrate through formalization that Boyer's explanatory model matches physics'
      <a href="/wiki/Epistemology" title="Epistemology">
       epistemology
      </a>
      in positing not directly observable entities as intermediaries.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ducasteljurgensen_53-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-ducasteljurgensen-53">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        53
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Anthropology" title="Anthropology">
       Anthropologist
      </a>
      Stewart Guthrie contends that people project human features onto non-human aspects of the world because it makes those aspects more familiar.
      <a href="/wiki/Sigmund_Freud" title="Sigmund Freud">
       Sigmund Freud
      </a>
      also suggested that god concepts are projections of one's father.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54">
       <a href="#cite_note-54">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        54
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">
         <span title="The material near this tag failed verification of its source citation(s). (March 2012)">
          not in citation given
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Likewise,
      <a href="/wiki/%C3%89mile_Durkheim" title="mile Durkheim">
       mile Durkheim
      </a>
      was one of the earliest to suggest that gods represent an extension of human social life to include supernatural beings. In line with this reasoning, psychologist Matt Rossano contends that when humans began living in larger groups, they may have created gods as a means of enforcing morality. In small groups, morality can be enforced by social forces such as gossip or reputation. However, it is much harder to enforce morality using social forces in much larger groups. Rossano indicates that by including ever-watchful gods and spirits, humans discovered an effective strategy for restraining selfishness and building more cooperative groups.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-supernature_55-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-supernature-55">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        55
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Existence_of_God">
       Existence of God
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Existence_of_God" title="Existence of God">
       Existence of God
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:St-thomas-aquinas.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4226" data-file-width="2813" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/St-thomas-aquinas.jpg/150px-St-thomas-aquinas.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/St-thomas-aquinas.jpg/225px-St-thomas-aquinas.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e3/St-thomas-aquinas.jpg/300px-St-thomas-aquinas.jpg 2x" width="150"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:St-thomas-aquinas.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Thomas_Aquinas" title="St. Thomas Aquinas">
         St. Thomas Aquinas
        </a>
        summarized
        <a href="/wiki/Quinque_viae" title="Quinque viae">
         five arguments
        </a>
        which he believed were proof for the existence of God
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      Countless arguments have been proposed in attempt to prove the existence of God.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56">
       <a href="#cite_note-56">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        56
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some of the most notable arguments are the
      <a href="/wiki/Quinque_viae" title="Quinque viae">
       Five Ways of Aquinas
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Argument_from_Desire" title="Argument from Desire">
       Argument from Desire
      </a>
      proposed by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/C.S._Lewis" title="C.S. Lewis">
       C.S. Lewis
      </a>
      , and the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ontological_Argument" title="Ontological Argument">
       Ontological Argument
      </a>
      formulated both by
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/St._Anselm" title="St. Anselm">
       St. Anselm
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Descartes" title="Descartes">
       Descartes
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57">
       <a href="#cite_note-57">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        57
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Even among
      <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">
       theists
      </a>
      , these proofs are debated, and some, such as the Ontological Argument, are highly controversial.
      <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Aquinas" title="Thomas Aquinas">
       Aquinas
      </a>
      spends a section of his treatise on God refuting St. Anselm's proof.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58">
       <a href="#cite_note-58">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        58
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      St. Anselm's approach was to define God as, "that than which nothing greater can be conceived". Famed pantheist philosopher
      <a href="/wiki/Baruch_Spinoza" title="Baruch Spinoza">
       Baruch Spinoza
      </a>
      would later carry this idea to its extreme: By God I understand a being absolutely infinite, i.e., a substance consisting of infinite attributes, of which each one expresses an eternal and infinite essence. For Spinoza, the whole of the natural universe is made of one substance, God, or its equivalent, Nature.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-59">
       <a href="#cite_note-59">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        59
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      His proof for the existence of God was a variation of the Ontological argument.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60">
       <a href="#cite_note-60">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        60
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Some theologians, such as the scientist and theologian
      <a href="/wiki/Alister_McGrath" title="Alister McGrath">
       A.E. McGrath
      </a>
      , argue that the existence of God is not a question that can be answered using the
      <a href="/wiki/Scientific_method" title="Scientific method">
       scientific method
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mcgrath2005_61-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-mcgrath2005-61">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        61
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-barackman2001_62-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-barackman2001-62">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        62
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agnostic" title="Agnostic">
       Agnostic
      </a>
      <a href="/wiki/Stephen_Jay_Gould" title="Stephen Jay Gould">
       Stephen Jay Gould
      </a>
      argues that science and religion are not in conflict and do not
      <a href="/wiki/Non-overlapping_magisteria" title="Non-overlapping magisteria">
       overlap
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63">
       <a href="#cite_note-63">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        63
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      There are many philosophical issues concerning the existence of God. Some definitions of God are nonspecific, while others can be self-contradictory. Arguments for the existence of God typically include metaphysical, empirical, inductive, and subjective types, while others revolve around the order and complexity in the world and perceived holes in evolutionary theory.
     </p>
     <p>
      Arguments against the existence of God typically include empirical, deductive, and inductive types. Conclusions reached include views that: "God does not exist" (
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_and_strong_atheism" title="Weak and strong atheism">
       strong atheism
      </a>
      ); "God almost certainly does not exist"
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dawkins_48-1">
       <a href="#cite_note-Dawkins-48">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        48
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      (
      <i>
       de facto
      </i>
      <a href="/wiki/Atheism" title="Atheism">
       atheism
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64">
       <a href="#cite_note-64">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        64
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ); "no one knows whether God exists" (
      <a href="/wiki/Agnosticism" title="Agnosticism">
       agnosticism
      </a>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65">
       <a href="#cite_note-65">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        65
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      ); "God exists, but this cannot be proven or disproven" (weak
      <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">
       theism
      </a>
      ); and that "God exists and this can be proven" (strong theism). There are numerous variations on these positions.
      <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">
       [
       <i>
        <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">
         <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">
          citation needed
         </span>
        </a>
       </i>
       ]
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Specific_attributes">
       Specific attributes
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Different religious traditions assign differing (though often similar) attributes and characteristics to God, including expansive powers and abilities, psychological characteristics, gender characteristics, and preferred nomenclature. The assignment of these attributes often differs according to the
      <a href="/wiki/Conceptions_of_God" title="Conceptions of God">
       conceptions of God
      </a>
      in the culture from which they arise. For example,
      <a href="/wiki/Attributes_of_God_in_Christianity" title="Attributes of God in Christianity">
       attributes of God in Christianity
      </a>
      , attributes of
      <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">
       God in Islam
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Thirteen_Attributes_of_Mercy" title="Thirteen Attributes of Mercy">
       the Thirteen Attributes of Mercy in Judaism
      </a>
      share certain similarities arising from their common roots.
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Names">
       Names
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God" title="Names of God">
       Names of God
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:99names_kuwait.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1575" data-file-width="2100" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/99names_kuwait.jpg/220px-99names_kuwait.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/99names_kuwait.jpg/330px-99names_kuwait.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/99names_kuwait.jpg/440px-99names_kuwait.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:99names_kuwait.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The 99
        <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Islam" title="Names of God in Islam">
         Names of God in Islam
        </a>
        , engraved into the ceiling of the
        <a href="/wiki/Grand_Mosque_(Kuwait)" title="Grand Mosque (Kuwait)">
         Grand Mosque of Kuwait
        </a>
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      The word
      <i>
       God
      </i>
      is one of the most complex and difficult in the English language. In the
      <a href="/wiki/Judeo-Christian" title="Judeo-Christian">
       Judeo-Christian
      </a>
      tradition, the Bible has been the principal source of the conceptions of God. That the Bible includes many different images, concepts, and ways of thinking about God has resulted in perpetual disagreements about how God is to be conceived and understood.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66">
       <a href="#cite_note-66">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        66
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Throughout the Hebrew and Christian Bibles there are many names for God. One of them is Elohim. Another one is
      <i>
       El Shaddai
      </i>
      , meaning "God Almighty".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67">
       <a href="#cite_note-67">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        67
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A third notable name is
      <i>
       El Elyon
      </i>
      , which means "The Most High God".
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68">
       <a href="#cite_note-68">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        68
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      God is described and referred in the
      <a href="/wiki/Quran" title="Quran">
       Quran
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">
       hadith
      </a>
      by certain names or attributes, the most common being
      <i>
       <a href="/wiki/R-%E1%B8%A4-M" title="R--M">
        Al-Rahman
       </a>
      </i>
      , meaning "Most Compassionate" and
      <i>
       Al-Rahim
      </i>
      , meaning "Most Merciful" (See
      <a href="/wiki/Names_of_God_in_Islam" title="Names of God in Islam">
       Names of God in Islam
      </a>
      ).
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ben_69-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Ben-69">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        69
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Vaishnavism" title="Vaishnavism">
       Vaishnavism
      </a>
      , a tradition in Hinduism, has
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_titles_and_names_of_Krishna" title="List of titles and names of Krishna">
       list of titles and names of Krishna
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Gender">
       Gender
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main article:
      <a href="/wiki/Gender_of_God" title="Gender of God">
       Gender of God
      </a>
     </div>
     <p>
      The gender of God can be viewed as a literal or as an
      <a href="/wiki/Allegory" title="Allegory">
       allegorical
      </a>
      aspect of a
      <a href="/wiki/Deity" title="Deity">
       deity
      </a>
      who, in Classical western philosophy, transcends bodily form.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70">
       <a href="#cite_note-70">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        70
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71">
       <a href="#cite_note-71">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        71
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      In
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Polytheistic" title="Polytheistic">
       polytheistic
      </a>
      religions,
      <i>
       the gods
      </i>
      are more likely to have literal sexual genders, allowing them to interact with each other and with humans in a sexual way. In most
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monotheistic" title="Monotheistic">
       monotheistic
      </a>
      religions, there is no comparable being for God to relate to in a literal gender-based way. Thus, in Classical western philosophy the
      <a href="/wiki/Gender" title="Gender">
       gender
      </a>
      of this one-and-only deity is most likely to be an
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Analogical" title="Analogical">
       analogical
      </a>
      statement of how humans and God address, and relate to, each other. Namely, God is seen as begetter of the world and revelation which corresponds to the active (as opposed to feminine receptive) role in sexual intercourse.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72">
       <a href="#cite_note-72">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        72
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      God is usually characterised as male in Biblical sources, except: female in
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Genesis&amp;verse=1:26-27&amp;src=KJV">
       Genesis 1:26-27
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73">
       <a href="#cite_note-73">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        73
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74">
       <a href="#cite_note-74">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        74
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Psalm&amp;verse=123:2-3&amp;src=KJV">
       Psalm 123:2-3
      </a>
      , and
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Luke&amp;verse=15:8-10&amp;src=KJV">
       Luke 15:8-10
      </a>
      ; a mother in
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Hosea&amp;verse=11:3-4&amp;src=KJV">
       Hosea 11:3-4
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Deuteronomy&amp;verse=32:18&amp;src=KJV">
       Deuteronomy 32:18
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Isaiah&amp;verse=66:13&amp;src=KJV">
       Isaiah 66:13
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Isaiah&amp;verse=49:15&amp;src=KJV">
       Isaiah 49:15
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Isaiah&amp;verse=42:14&amp;src=KJV">
       Isaiah 42:14
      </a>
      ,
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Psalm&amp;verse=131:2&amp;src=KJV">
       Psalm 131:2
      </a>
      ; a mother eagle in
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Deuteronomy&amp;verse=32:11-12&amp;src=KJV">
       Deuteronomy 32:11-12
      </a>
      ; and a mother hen in
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Matthew&amp;verse=23:37&amp;src=KJV">
       Matthew 23:37
      </a>
      and
      <a class="external text" href="http://tools.wmflabs.org/bibleversefinder/?book=Luke&amp;verse=13:34&amp;src=KJV">
       Luke 13:34
      </a>
      .
     </p>
     <h3>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Relationship_with_creation">
       Relationship with creation
      </span>
     </h3>
     <div class="hatnote">
      See also:
      <a href="/wiki/Creator_deity" title="Creator deity">
       Creator deity
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">
       Prayer
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">
       Worship
      </a>
     </div>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cima_da_Conegliano,_God_the_Father.jpg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="544" data-file-width="700" height="171" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Cima_da_Conegliano%2C_God_the_Father.jpg/220px-Cima_da_Conegliano%2C_God_the_Father.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Cima_da_Conegliano%2C_God_the_Father.jpg/330px-Cima_da_Conegliano%2C_God_the_Father.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/Cima_da_Conegliano%2C_God_the_Father.jpg/440px-Cima_da_Conegliano%2C_God_the_Father.jpg 2x" width="220"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cima_da_Conegliano,_God_the_Father.jpg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        <i>
         God the Father
        </i>
        by
        <a href="/wiki/Cima_da_Conegliano" title="Cima da Conegliano">
         Cima da Conegliano
        </a>
        , c. 1515
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <p>
      <a href="/wiki/Prayer" title="Prayer">
       Prayer
      </a>
      plays a significant role among many believers. Muslims believe that the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Purpose_of_life" title="Purpose of life">
       purpose of existence
      </a>
      is to
      <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">
       worship
      </a>
      God.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-patheos1_75-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-patheos1-75">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        75
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76">
       <a href="#cite_note-76">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        76
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      He is viewed as a personal God and there are no intermediaries, such as
      <a href="/wiki/Clergy" title="Clergy">
       clergy
      </a>
      , to contact God. Prayer often also includes
      <a href="/wiki/Supplication" title="Supplication">
       supplication
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Forgiveness#Religious_views" title="Forgiveness">
       asking forgiveness
      </a>
      . God is often believed to be forgiving. For example, a
      <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">
       hadith
      </a>
      states God would replace a sinless people with one who sinned but still asked repentance.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77">
       <a href="#cite_note-77">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        77
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Christian theologian
      <a href="/wiki/Alister_McGrath" title="Alister McGrath">
       Alister McGrath
      </a>
      writes that there are good reasons to suggest that a "personal god" is integral to the Christian outlook, but that one has to understand it is an analogy. "To say that God is like a person is to affirm the divine ability and willingness to relate to others. This does not imply that God is human, or located at a specific point in the universe."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78">
       <a href="#cite_note-78">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        78
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Adherents of different religions generally disagree as to how to best
      <a href="/wiki/Worship" title="Worship">
       worship
      </a>
      God and what is
      <a href="/wiki/Divine_providence" title="Divine providence">
       God's plan
      </a>
      for mankind, if there is one. There are different approaches to reconciling the contradictory claims of monotheistic religions. One view is taken by exclusivists, who believe they are the
      <a href="/wiki/Chosen_people" title="Chosen people">
       chosen people
      </a>
      or have exclusive access to
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Absolute_truth" title="Absolute truth">
       absolute truth
      </a>
      , generally through
      <a href="/wiki/Revelation" title="Revelation">
       revelation
      </a>
      or encounter with the Divine, which adherents of other religions do not. Another view is
      <a href="/wiki/Religious_pluralism" title="Religious pluralism">
       religious pluralism
      </a>
      . A pluralist typically believes that his religion is the right one, but does not deny the partial truth of other religions. An example of a pluralist view in Christianity is
      <a href="/wiki/Supersessionism" title="Supersessionism">
       supersessionism
      </a>
      , i.e., the belief that one's religion is the fulfillment of previous religions. A third approach is
      <a href="/wiki/Inclusivism" title="Inclusivism">
       relativistic inclusivism
      </a>
      , where everybody is seen as equally right; an example being
      <a href="/wiki/Universalism" title="Universalism">
       universalism
      </a>
      : the doctrine that
      <a href="/wiki/Salvation" title="Salvation">
       salvation
      </a>
      is eventually available for everyone. A fourth approach is
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Syncretic" title="Syncretic">
       syncretism
      </a>
      , mixing different elements from different religions. An example of syncretism is the
      <a href="/wiki/New_Age" title="New Age">
       New Age
      </a>
      movement.
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Theological_approaches">
       Theological approaches
      </span>
     </h2>
     <p>
      Theologians and philosophers have ascribed a number of attributes to God, including
      <a href="/wiki/Omniscience" title="Omniscience">
       omniscience
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Omnipotence" title="Omnipotence">
       omnipotence
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Omnipresence" title="Omnipresence">
       omnipresence
      </a>
      , perfect
      <a href="/wiki/Good_and_evil" title="Good and evil">
       goodness
      </a>
      , divine
      <a href="/wiki/Simplicity" title="Simplicity">
       simplicity
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Eternity" title="Eternity">
       eternal
      </a>
      and
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Necessary_Existent" title="Necessary Existent">
       necessary
      </a>
      existence. God has been described as
      <a href="/wiki/Incorporeality" title="Incorporeality">
       incorporeal
      </a>
      , a personal being, the source of all
      <a href="/wiki/Moral_obligation" title="Moral obligation">
       moral obligation
      </a>
      , and the greatest conceivable being existent.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Swinburne_1-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Swinburne-1">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        1
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      These attributes were all claimed to varying degrees by the early
      <a href="/wiki/Judaism" title="Judaism">
       Jewish
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Christianity" title="Christianity">
       Christian
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">
       Muslim
      </a>
      scholars, including
      <a href="/wiki/Augustine_of_Hippo" title="Augustine of Hippo">
       St Augustine
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_45-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Edwards-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <a href="/wiki/Al-Ghazali" title="Al-Ghazali">
       Al-Ghazali
      </a>
      ,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_79-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-79">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        79
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Maimonides" title="Maimonides">
       Maimonides
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Edwards_45-3">
       <a href="#cite_note-Edwards-45">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        45
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Many
      <a href="/wiki/Medieval_philosophy" title="Medieval philosophy">
       medieval philosophers
      </a>
      developed arguments for the existence of God,
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Platinga_2-2">
       <a href="#cite_note-Platinga-2">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        2
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      while attempting to comprehend the precise implications of God's attributes. Reconciling some of those attributes generated important philosophical problems and debates. For example, God's omniscience may seem to imply that God knows how free agents will choose to act. If God does know this, their apparent
      <a href="/wiki/Free_will" title="Free will">
       free will
      </a>
      might be illusory, or foreknowledge does not imply predestination; and if God does not know it, God may not be omniscient.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wierenga_80-0">
       <a href="#cite_note-Wierenga-80">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        80
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      However, if by its essential nature, free will is not predetermined, then the effect of its will can never be perfectly predicted by anyone, regardless of intelligence and knowledge. Although knowledge of the options presented to that will, combined with perfect-infinite intelligence, could be said to provide God with omniscience if omniscience is defined as knowledge or understanding of all that is.
     </p>
     <p>
      The last centuries of philosophy have seen vigorous questions regarding the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Arguments_for_the_existence_of_God" title="Arguments for the existence of God">
       arguments for God's existence
      </a>
      raised by such philosophers as
      <a href="/wiki/Immanuel_Kant" title="Immanuel Kant">
       Immanuel Kant
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/David_Hume" title="David Hume">
       David Hume
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Antony_Flew" title="Antony Flew">
       Antony Flew
      </a>
      , although Kant held that the
      <a href="/wiki/Argument_from_morality" title="Argument from morality">
       argument from morality
      </a>
      was valid. The
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Theist" title="Theist">
       theist
      </a>
      response has been either to contend, like
      <a href="/wiki/Alvin_Plantinga" title="Alvin Plantinga">
       Alvin Plantinga
      </a>
      , that faith is "
      <a href="/wiki/Reformed_epistemology" title="Reformed epistemology">
       properly basic
      </a>
      "; or to take, like
      <a href="/wiki/Richard_Swinburne" title="Richard Swinburne">
       Richard Swinburne
      </a>
      , the
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Evidentialist" title="Evidentialist">
       evidentialist
      </a>
      position.
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81">
       <a href="#cite_note-81">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        81
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      Some
      <a href="/wiki/Theism" title="Theism">
       theists
      </a>
      agree that none of the arguments for God's existence are compelling, but argue that
      <a href="/wiki/Faith" title="Faith">
       faith
      </a>
      is not a product of
      <a href="/wiki/Reason" title="Reason">
       reason
      </a>
      , but requires risk. There would be no risk, they say, if the arguments for God's existence were as solid as the laws of logic, a position summed up by
      <a href="/wiki/Blaise_Pascal" title="Blaise Pascal">
       Pascal
      </a>
      as: "The heart has reasons which reason knows not of."
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82">
       <a href="#cite_note-82">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        82
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      A recent theory utilizing concepts from physics and neurophysiology proposes that God can be conceptualized within the theory of
      <a href="/wiki/Integrative_level" title="Integrative level">
       integrative level
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83">
       <a href="#cite_note-83">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        83
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <p>
      Most major religions hold God not as a metaphor, but a being that influences our day-to-day existences. Many believers allow for the existence of other, less powerful
      <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spiritual_being" title="Spiritual being">
       spiritual beings
      </a>
      , and give them names such as
      <a href="/wiki/Angel" title="Angel">
       angels
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Saint" title="Saint">
       saints
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Jinn" title="Jinn">
       jinns
      </a>
      ,
      <a href="/wiki/Demon" title="Demon">
       demons
      </a>
      , and
      <a href="/wiki/Deva_(New_Age)" title="Deva (New Age)">
       devas
      </a>
      .
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84">
       <a href="#cite_note-84">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        84
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85">
       <a href="#cite_note-85">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        85
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86">
       <a href="#cite_note-86">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        86
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87">
       <a href="#cite_note-87">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        87
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
      <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88">
       <a href="#cite_note-88">
        <span>
         [
        </span>
        88
        <span>
         ]
        </span>
       </a>
      </sup>
     </p>
     <h2>
      <span class="mw-headline" id="Distribution_of_belief_in_God">
       Distribution of belief in God
      </span>
     </h2>
     <div class="thumb tright">
      <div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;">
       <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Europe_belief_in_god.svg">
        <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1556" data-file-width="1700" height="229" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Europe_belief_in_god.svg/250px-Europe_belief_in_god.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Europe_belief_in_god.svg/375px-Europe_belief_in_god.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/47/Europe_belief_in_god.svg/500px-Europe_belief_in_god.svg.png 2x" width="250"/>
       </a>
       <div class="thumbcaption">
        <div class="magnify">
         <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Europe_belief_in_god.svg" title="Enlarge">
         </a>
        </div>
        The percentage of population in European countries who responded in a 2005 survey that they "believe there is a God".
       </div>
      </div>
     </div>
     <div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">
      Main articles:
      <a href="/wiki/List_of_religious_populations" title="List of religious populations">
       List of religious populations
      </a>
      and
      <a href="/wiki/Irreligion_by_country" title="Irreligion by country">
       Irreligion by country
      </a>
     </div>

      </span>
     </h2>

       </a>
      </li>

       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/List_of_deities" title="List of deities">

       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheon_(gods)" title="Pantheon (gods)">

       <li id="cite_note-Lataster-14">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-Lataster_14-0">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation book">
          Raphael Lataster (2013).
          <i>
           There was no Jesus, there is no God: A Scholarly Examination of the Scientific, Historical, and Philosophical Evidence &amp; Arguments for Monotheism
          </i>
          . p.165.
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1492234419" title="Special:BookSources/1492234419">
           1492234419
          </a>
          .
          <q>
           This one god could be of the deistic or pantheistic sort. Deism might be superior in explaining why God has seemingly left us to our own devices and pantheism could be the more logical option as it fits well with the ontological argument's 'maximally-great entity' and doesn't rely on unproven concepts about 'nothing' (as in 'creation out of nothing'). A mixture of the two, pandeism, could be the most likely God-concept of all.
          </q>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.aulast=Raphael+Lataster&amp;rft.au=Raphael+Lataster&amp;rft.btitle=There+was+no+Jesus%2C+there+is+no+God%3A+A+Scholarly+Examination+of+the+Scientific%2C+Historical%2C+and+Philosophical+Evidence+%26+Arguments+for+Monotheism&amp;rft.date=2013&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=1492234419&amp;rft.pages=165&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-Dawe-15">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-Dawe_15-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-Dawe_15-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation book">
          Alan H. Dawe (2011).
          <i>
           The God Franchise: A Theory of Everything
          </i>
          . p.48.
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0473201143" title="Special:BookSources/0473201143">
           0473201143
          </a>
          .
          <q>
           Pandeism: This is the belief that God created the universe, is now one with it, and so, is no longer a separate conscious entity. This is a combination of pantheism (God is identical to the universe) and deism (God created the universe and then withdrew Himself).
          </q>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.au=Alan+H.+Dawe&amp;rft.aulast=Alan+H.+Dawe&amp;rft.btitle=The+God+Franchise%3A+A+Theory+of+Everything&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0473201143&amp;rft.pages=48&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
