       <li id="cite_note-18">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-18">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The ulterior etymology is disputed. Apart from the unlikely hypothesis of adoption from a foreign tongue, the OTeut. "ghuba" implies as its preTeut-type either "*ghodho-m" or "*ghodto-m". The former does not appear to admit of explanation; but the latter would represent the neut. pple. of a root "gheu-". There are two Aryan roots of the required form ("*g,heu-" with palatal aspirate) one with meaning 'to invoke' (Skr. "hu") the other 'to pour, to offer sacrifice' (Skr "hu", Gr. i;, OE "geotn" Yete v).
         <a href="/wiki/Oxford_English_Dictionary" title="Oxford English Dictionary">
          OED Compact Edition, G, p. 267
         </a>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-BARNHART323-19">

         <i>
          The Barnhart Concise Dictionary of Etymology: the Origins of American English Words
         </i>
         , page 323.
         <a href="/wiki/HarperCollins" title="HarperCollins">
          HarperCollins
         </a>
         .
         <a class="internal mw-magiclink-isbn" href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0062700847">
          ISBN 0-06-270084-7

       <li id="cite_note-20">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-20">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <a href="/wiki/Webster%27s_New_World_Dictionary" title="Webster's New World Dictionary">
          Webster's New World Dictionary
         </a>
         ; "God n. ME &lt; OE, akin to Ger gott, Goth guth, prob. &lt; IE base * hau-, to call out to, invoke &gt; Sans havat, (he) calls upon; 1. any of various beings conceived of as supernatural, immortal, and having special powers over the lives and affairs of people and the course of nature; deity, esp. a male deity: typically considered objects of worship; 2. an image that is worshiped; idol 3. a person or thing deified or excessively honored and admired; 4. [G-] in monotheistic religions, the creator and ruler of the universe, regarded as eternal, infinite, all-powerful, and all-knowing;
         <a href="/wiki/Supreme_Being" title="Supreme Being">
          Supreme Being
         </a>
         ; the Almighty"
        </span>

       <li id="cite_note-21">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>

           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <a class="external text" href="http://dictionary.reference.com/browse/God" rel="nofollow">
          Dictionary.com
         </a>
         ; "God /gd/ noun: 1. the one Supreme Being, the creator and ruler of the universe. 2. the Supreme Being considered with reference to a particular attribute. 3. (lowercase) one of several deities, esp. a male deity, presiding over some portion of worldly affairs. 4. (often lowercase) a supreme being according to some particular conception: the God of mercy. 5. Christian Science. the Supreme Being, understood as Life, Truth, Love, Mind, Soul, Spirit, Principle. 6. (lowercase) an image of a deity; an idol. 7. (lowercase) any deified person or object. 8. (often lowercase) Gods, Theater. 8a. the upper balcony in a theater. 8b. the spectators in this part of the balcony."
        </span>

       <li id="cite_note-TFDtheism-38">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-TFDtheism_38-0">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation web">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.thefreedictionary.com/theism" rel="nofollow">

         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.btitle=Theism+%E2%80%93+definition+of+theism+by+the+Free+Online+Dictionary%2C+Thesaurus+and+Encyclopedia&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.thefreedictionary.com%2Ftheism&amp;rft.pub=TheFreeDictionary.com&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-39">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-39">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation book">
          Sean F. Johnston (2009).
          <i>
           The History of Science: A Beginner's Guide
          </i>
          . p.90.
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1-85168-681-9" title="Special:BookSources/1-85168-681-9">
           1-85168-681-9
          </a>
          .
          <q>
           In its most abstract form, deism may not attempt to describe the characteristics of such a non-interventionist creator, or even that the universe is identical with God (a variant known as pandeism).
          </q>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.aulast=Sean+F.+Johnston&amp;rft.au=Sean+F.+Johnston&amp;rft.btitle=The+History+of+Science%3A+A+Beginner%27s+Guide&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=1-85168-681-9&amp;rft.pages=90&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-40">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-40">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation book">
          Paul Bradley (2011).
          <i>
           This Strange Eventful History: A Philosophy of Meaning
          </i>
          . p.156.
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/0875868762" title="Special:BookSources/0875868762">
           0875868762
          </a>
          .
          <q>
           Pandeism combines the concepts of Deism and Pantheism with a god who creates the universe and then becomes it.
          </q>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.aulast=Paul+Bradley&amp;rft.au=Paul+Bradley&amp;rft.btitle=This+Strange+Eventful+History%3A+A+Philosophy+of+Meaning&amp;rft.date=2011&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0875868762&amp;rft.pages=156&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-Fuller-41">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         ^
         <a href="#cite_ref-Fuller_41-0">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             a
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
         <a href="#cite_ref-Fuller_41-1">
          <sup>
           <i>
            <b>
             b
            </b>
           </i>
          </sup>
         </a>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation book">
          Allan R. Fuller (2010).
          <i>
           Thought: The Only Reality
          </i>
          . p.79.
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1608445909" title="Special:BookSources/1608445909">
           1608445909
          </a>
          .
          <q>
           Pandeism is another belief that states that God is identical to the universe, but God no longer exists in a way where He can be contacted; therefore, this theory can only be proven to exist by reason. Pandeism views the entire universe as being from God and now the universe is the entirety of God, but the universe at some point in time will fold back into one single being which is God Himself that created all. Pandeism raises the question as to why would God create a universe and then abandon it? As this relates to pantheism, it raises the question of how did the universe come about what is its aim and purpose?
          </q>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.au=Allan+R.+Fuller&amp;rft.aulast=Allan+R.+Fuller&amp;rft.btitle=Thought%3A+The+Only+Reality&amp;rft.date=2010&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=1608445909&amp;rft.pages=79&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-42">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-42">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <span class="citation book">
          Peter C. Rogers (2009).
          <i>
           Ultimate Truth, Book 1
          </i>
          . p.121.
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/1438979681" title="Special:BookSources/1438979681">
           1438979681
          </a>
          .
          <q>
           As with Panentheism, Pantheism is derived from the Greek: 'pan'= all and 'theos' = God, it literally means God is All and All is God. Pantheist purports that everything is part of an all-inclusive, indwelling, intangible God; or that the Universe, or nature, and God are the same. Further review helps to accentuate the idea that natural law, existence, and the Universe which is the sum total of all that is, was, and shall be, is represented in the theological principle of an abstract 'god' rather than an individual, creative Divine Being or Beings of any kind. This is the key element which distinguishes them from Panentheists and Pandeists. As such, although many religions may claim to hold Pantheistic elements, they are more commonly Panentheistic or Pandeistic in nature.
          </q>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.aulast=Peter+C.+Rogers&amp;rft.au=Peter+C.+Rogers&amp;rft.btitle=Ultimate+Truth%2C+Book+1&amp;rft.date=2009&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=1438979681&amp;rft.pages=121&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>

       <li id="cite_note-46">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">

         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.aufirst=Stephen+J.&amp;rft.au=Gould%2C+Stephen+J.&amp;rft.aulast=Gould&amp;rft.btitle=Leonardo%27s+Mountain+of+Clams+and+the+Diet+of+Worms&amp;rft.date=1998&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=0-224-05043-5&amp;rft.pages=274&amp;rft.pub=Jonathan+Cape&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-64">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-64">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         The word
         <i>
          atheism
         </i>
         originated from the Greek
         <span lang="grc">
          <a class="extiw" href="//en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%E1%BC%84%CE%B8%CE%B5%CE%BF%CF%82" title="wikt:">
           
          </a>
         </span>
         (
         <i>
          atheos
         </i>
         ), meaning "without god(s)".
        </span>
       </li>
       <li id="cite_note-65">
        <span class="mw-cite-backlink">
         <b>
          <a href="#cite_ref-65">
           ^
          </a>
         </b>
        </span>
        <span class="reference-text">
         <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Henry_Huxley" title="Thomas Henry Huxley">
          Thomas Henry Huxley
         </a>
         , an English biologist, was the first to come up with the word
         <i>
          agnostic
         </i>
         in 1869
         <span class="citation book">
          Dixon, Thomas (2008).
          <i>
           Science and Religion: A Very Short Introduction
          </i>
          . Oxford: Oxford University Press. p.63.
          <a href="/wiki/International_Standard_Book_Number" title="International Standard Book Number">
           ISBN
          </a>
          <a href="/wiki/Special:BookSources/978-0-19-929551-7" title="Special:BookSources/978-0-19-929551-7">
           978-0-19-929551-7
          </a>
          .
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.au=Dixon%2C+Thomas&amp;rft.aufirst=Thomas&amp;rft.aulast=Dixon&amp;rft.btitle=Science+and+Religion%3A+A+Very+Short+Introduction&amp;rft.date=2008&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.isbn=978-0-19-929551-7&amp;rft.pages=63&amp;rft.place=Oxford&amp;rft.pub=Oxford+University+Press&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
         However, earlier authors and published works have promoted an agnostic points of view. They include
         <a href="/wiki/Protagoras" title="Protagoras">
          Protagoras
         </a>
         , a 5th-century
         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Before_Common_Era" title="Before Common Era">
          BCE
         </a>
         Greek philosopher.
         <span class="citation web">
          <a class="external text" href="http://www.iep.utm.edu/p/protagor.htm" rel="nofollow">
           "The Internet Encyclopedia of Philosophy - Protagoras (c. 490 - c. 420 BCE)"
          </a>
          .
          <a class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20081014181706/http://www.iep.utm.edu/p/protagor.htm" rel="nofollow">
           Archived
          </a>
          from the original on 2008-10-14
          <span class="reference-accessdate">
           . Retrieved
           <span class="nowrap">
            2008-10-06
           </span>
          </span>
          .
          <q>
           While the pious might wish to look to the gods to provide absolute moral guidance in the relativistic universe of the Sophistic Enlightenment, that certainty also was cast into doubt by philosophic and sophistic thinkers, who pointed out the absurdity and immorality of the conventional epic accounts of the gods. Protagoras' prose treatise about the gods began 'Concerning the gods, I have no means of knowing whether they exist or not or of what sort they may be. Many things prevent knowledge including the obscurity of the subject and the brevity of human life.'
          </q>
         </span>
         <span class="Z3988" title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AGod&amp;rft.btitle=The+Internet+Encyclopedia+of+Philosophy+-+Protagoras+%28c.+490+-+c.+420+BCE%29&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fwww.iep.utm.edu%2Fp%2Fprotagor.htm&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook">
          <span style="display:none;">
          </span>
         </span>
        </span>
       </li>

         </a>
         .
         <q>
          The encyclopedia will contain articles on all the religions of the world and on all the great systems of ethics. It will aim at containing articles on every religious belief or custom, and on every ethical movement, every philosophical idea, every moral practice.
